The total number of [Israeli settlers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_settlement "Israeli settlement") beyond the [Green Line](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_Line_(Israel) "Green Line (Israel)") is over 600,000 (≈10% of the Jewish Israeli population).<sup id="cite_ref-610"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-610">[601]</a></sup> In 2016, 399,300 Israelis [lived](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_statistics_for_Israeli_settlements_in_the_West_Bank "Population statistics for Israeli settlements in the West Bank") in [West Bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Bank "West Bank") settlements,<sup id="cite_ref-districts_pop_359-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-districts_pop-359">[350]</a></sup> including those that predated the establishment of the State of Israel and which were re-established after the [Six-Day War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Six-Day_War "Six-Day War"), in cities such as [Hebron](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebron "Hebron") and [Gush Etzion](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gush_Etzion "Gush Etzion") bloc. Additionally there were more than 200,000 Jews living in [East Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Jerusalem "East Jerusalem"),<sup id="cite_ref-jerusalem_pop_360-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-jerusalem_pop-360">[351]</a></sup> and 22,000 in the [Golan Heights](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golan_Heights "Golan Heights").<sup id="cite_ref-districts_pop_359-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-districts_pop-359">[350]</a></sup> Approximately 7,800 Israelis [lived in settlements](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_statistics_for_Israeli_Gaza_Strip_settlements "Population statistics for Israeli Gaza Strip settlements") in the Gaza Strip, known as [Gush Katif](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gush_Katif "Gush Katif"), until they were evacuated by the government as part of its 2005 [disengagement plan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel%27s_unilateral_disengagement_plan "Israel's unilateral disengagement plan").<sup id="cite_ref-611"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-611">[602]</a></sup>

Israeli Arabs (including the Arab population of East Jerusalem and the Golan Heights) comprise 21.1% of the population or 1,995,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-612"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-612">[603]</a></sup> In a 2017 poll, 40% of Arab citizens of Israel identified as "Arab in Israel" or "Arab citizen of Israel", 15% identified as "Palestinian", 8.9% as "Palestinian in Israel" or "Palestinian citizen of Israel", and 8.7% as "Arab"; a poll found that 60% of Israeli Arabs have a positive view of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-613"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-613">[604]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-614"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-614">[605]</a></sup>

### Major urban areas

Israel has four major metropolitan areas: [Gush Dan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gush_Dan "Gush Dan") (Tel Aviv metropolitan area; population 3,854,000), [Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem_metropolitan_area "Jerusalem metropolitan area") (population 1,253,900), [Haifa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haifa_metropolitan_area "Haifa metropolitan area") (924,400), and [Beersheba](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beersheba_metropolitan_area "Beersheba metropolitan area") (377,100).<sup id="cite_ref-615"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-615">[606]</a></sup>

Israel's largest municipality, in population and area, is [Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem "Jerusalem") with 981,711 residents in an area of 125 square kilometres (48 sq mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Israelpopulations_616-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-Israelpopulations-616">[607]</a></sup> Israeli government statistics on Jerusalem include the population and area of [East Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Jerusalem "East Jerusalem"), the status of which is in international dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-617"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-617">[608]</a></sup> [Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Aviv "Tel Aviv") and [Haifa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haifa "Haifa") rank as Israel's next most populous cities, with populations of 474,530 and 290,306, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Israelpopulations_616-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-Israelpopulations-616">[607]</a></sup> The (mainly [Haredi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haredi "Haredi")) city of [Bnei Brak](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bnei_Brak "Bnei Brak") is the most densely populated city in Israel and one of the [10 most densely populated cities](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_cities_proper_by_population_density "List of cities proper by population density") in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-618"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-618">[609]</a></sup>

Israel has 16 [cities](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Israel "List of cities in Israel") with populations over 100,000. As of 2018 there are 77 Israeli localities granted ["municipalities" (or "city") status](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/City_council_(Israel) "City council (Israel)") by the Ministry of the Interior,<sup id="cite_ref-619"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-619">[610]</a></sup> [four of which are in the West Bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Israeli_settlements_with_city_status_in_the_West_Bank "List of Israeli settlements with city status in the West Bank").<sup id="cite_ref-620"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-620">[611]</a></sup>

| 10 | [Holon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holon "Holon") | [Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Aviv_District "Tel Aviv District") | 197,957 | 20 | [Lod](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lod "Lod") | [Central](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_District_(Israel) "Central District (Israel)") | 85,351 |

<small><span id="endnote_jerusalem"><b><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#ref_jerusalem">^a</a></b> This number includes <a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Jerusalem" title="East Jerusalem">East Jerusalem</a> and <a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Bank" title="West Bank">West Bank</a> areas, which had a total population of 573,330 inhabitants in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-621"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-621">[612]</a></sup> Israeli sovereignty over East Jerusalem is <a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Status_of_Jerusalem" title="Status of Jerusalem">internationally unrecognized</a>.</span></small>

### Language

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:%D7%A9%D7%9C%D7%98_%D7%A8%D7%97%D7%95%D7%91_%D7%99%D7%94%D7%95%D7%93%D7%94_(3777232251).jpg)

[Road sign](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Road_signs_in_Israel "Road signs in Israel") in [Hebrew](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language "Hebrew language"), Arabic, and English

Israel's official language is [Hebrew](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language "Hebrew language"). Hebrew is the primary language of the state and is spoken daily by the majority of the population. Prior to 1948, [opposition](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Yiddish_sentiment "Anti-Yiddish sentiment") to [Yiddish](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yiddish "Yiddish"), the historical language of the Ashkenazi Jews, was common among supporters of the Zionist movement, including the Yishuv, who sought to promote [Hebrew's revival](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language "Revival of the Hebrew language") as a unifying national language.<sup id="cite_ref-622"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-622">[613]</a></sup> These sentiments were reflected in the early policies of the Israeli government, which largely banned [Yiddish theatre performances](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yiddish_theatre "Yiddish theatre") and publications.<sup id="cite_ref-623"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-623">[614]</a></sup> Until 2018, [Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language_in_Israel "Arabic language in Israel") was also an official language of Israel;<sup id="cite_ref-lang1_13-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-lang1-13">[11]</a></sup> in 2018 [it was downgraded](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_Law:_Israel_as_the_Nation-State_of_the_Jewish_People "Basic Law: Israel as the Nation-State of the Jewish People") to having a 'special status in the state'.<sup id="cite_ref-lang2_11-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-lang2-11">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lang3_12-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-lang3-12">[10]</a></sup> Arabic is spoken by the Arab minority, with Hebrew taught in Arab schools.

Due to mass immigration from the former Soviet Union and [Ethiopia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aliyah_from_Ethiopia "Aliyah from Ethiopia") (some 130,000 [Ethiopian Jews live in Israel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethiopian_Jews_in_Israel "Ethiopian Jews in Israel")),<sup id="cite_ref-The_Ethiopian_Population_In_Israel_624-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-The_Ethiopian_Population_In_Israel-624">[615]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-625"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-625">[616]</a></sup> [Russian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_language_in_Israel "Russian language in Israel") and [Amharic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amharic "Amharic") are widely spoken.<sup id="cite_ref-626"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-626">[617]</a></sup> Over one million Russian-speaking immigrants [arrived](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1990s_Post-Soviet_aliyah "1990s Post-Soviet aliyah") in Israel between 1990 and 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-627"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-627">[618]</a></sup> French is spoken by around 700,000 Israelis,<sup id="cite_ref-628"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-628">[619]</a></sup> mostly originating [from France](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Jews_in_Israel "French Jews in Israel") and North Africa (see [Maghrebi Jews](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maghrebi_Jews "Maghrebi Jews")). English was an official language during the Mandate period; it lost this status after the establishment of Israel, but retains a role comparable to that of an official language.<sup id="cite_ref-629"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-629">[620]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-630"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-630">[621]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-631"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-631">[622]</a></sup> Many Israelis communicate reasonably well in English, as many television programmes are broadcast in English with [subtitles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subtitles "Subtitles") and the language is taught from the early grades in elementary school. Israeli universities offer courses in the English language on various subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-632"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-632">[623]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2023)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

### Religion

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Westernwall2.jpg)

The [Dome of the Rock](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock "Dome of the Rock") and the [Western Wall](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Wall "Western Wall"), Jerusalem

The religious affiliation of the Israeli population as of 2022 was 73.6% Jewish, 18.1% [Muslim](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslims "Muslims"), 1.9% [Christian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christians "Christians"), and 1.6% [Druze](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druze "Druze"). The remaining 4.8% included faiths such as [Samaritanism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samaritanism "Samaritanism") and [Baháʼí](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith "Baháʼí Faith"), as well as "religiously unclassified".<sup id="cite_ref-CBS_2.1_633-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-CBS_2.1-633">[624]</a></sup>

The [religious affiliation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_religious_movements "Jewish religious movements") of [Israeli Jews](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_Jews "Israeli Jews") varies widely: a 2016 survey by [Pew Research](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pew_Research "Pew Research") indicates that 49% self-identify as [Hiloni](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hiloni "Hiloni") (secular), 29% as [Masorti](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masortim "Masortim") (traditional), 13% as [Dati](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dati "Dati") (religious) and 9% as [Haredi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haredi_Judaism "Haredi Judaism") (ultra-Orthodox).<sup id="cite_ref-634"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-634">[625]</a></sup> Haredi Jews are expected to represent over 20% of Israel's Jewish population by 2028.<sup id="cite_ref-635"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-635">[626]</a></sup>

[Muslims](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam_in_Israel "Islam in Israel") constitute Israel's largest religious minority, making up about 17.6% of the population. About 2% of the population is [Christian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity_in_Israel "Christianity in Israel") and 1.6% is [Druze](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druze_in_Israel "Druze in Israel").<sup id="cite_ref-cia_314-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-cia-314">[305]</a></sup> The Christian population comprises primarily [Arab Christians](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Christians "Arab Christians") and [Aramean Christians](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arameans_in_Israel "Arameans in Israel"), but also includes post-Soviet immigrants, foreign laborers, and followers of [Messianic Judaism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Messianic_Judaism "Messianic Judaism"), considered by most Christians and Jews to be a form of Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-636"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-636">[627]</a></sup> Members of many other religious groups, including [Buddhists](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buddhism "Buddhism") and [Hindus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinduism_in_Israel "Hinduism in Israel"), maintain a presence in Israel, albeit in small numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-637"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-637">[628]</a></sup> Out of over one million [immigrants](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1990s_Post-Soviet_aliyah "1990s Post-Soviet aliyah") from the former Soviet Union, about 300,000 are considered not Jewish by the [Chief Rabbinate of Israel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chief_Rabbinate_of_Israel "Chief Rabbinate of Israel").<sup id="cite_ref-638"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-638">[629]</a></sup>

Israel comprises a major part of the [Holy Land](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holy_Land "Holy Land"), a region of significant importance to all [Abrahamic religions](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abrahamic_religions "Abrahamic religions"). The city of [Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem "Jerusalem") is of [special importance](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religious_significance_of_Jerusalem "Religious significance of Jerusalem") to Jews, Muslims, and Christians, as it is the home of [sites](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_places_in_Jerusalem "List of places in Jerusalem") that are pivotal to their religious beliefs, such as the [Old City](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_City_(Jerusalem) "Old City (Jerusalem)") that incorporates the [Western Wall](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Wall "Western Wall") and the [Temple Mount](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temple_Mount "Temple Mount") (Al-Aqsa Mosque compound) and the [Church of the Holy Sepulchre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre "Church of the Holy Sepulchre").<sup id="cite_ref-639"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-639">[630]</a></sup> Other locations of religious importance are [Nazareth](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazareth "Nazareth") (site of the [Annunciation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annunciation "Annunciation") of [Mary](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus) "Mary (mother of Jesus)")), [Tiberias](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tiberias "Tiberias") and [Safed](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safed "Safed") (two of the [Four Holy Cities](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Four_Holy_Cities "Four Holy Cities") in Judaism), the [White Mosque](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_Mosque,_Ramla "White Mosque, Ramla") in [Ramla](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramla "Ramla") (shrine of the prophet [Saleh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salih "Salih")), and the [Church of Saint George and Mosque of Al-Khadr, Lod](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_of_Saint_George_and_Mosque_of_Al-Khadr,_Lod "Church of Saint George and Mosque of Al-Khadr, Lod") (tomb of [Saint George](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint_George "Saint George") or [Al Khidr](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al_Khidr "Al Khidr")). A number of other religious landmarks are located in the [West Bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Bank "West Bank"), including [Joseph's Tomb](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph%27s_Tomb "Joseph's Tomb"), the [birthplace of Jesus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_of_the_Nativity "Church of the Nativity"), [Rachel's Tomb](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rachel%27s_Tomb "Rachel's Tomb"), and the [Cave of the Patriarchs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cave_of_the_Patriarchs "Cave of the Patriarchs"). The [administrative center](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arc_(Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD) "Arc (Baháʼí)") of the [Baháʼí Faith](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith "Baháʼí Faith") and the [Shrine of the Báb](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shrine_of_the_B%C3%A1b "Shrine of the Báb") are located at the [Baháʼí World Centre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_World_Centre "Baháʼí World Centre") in [Haifa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haifa "Haifa"); the leader of the faith is [buried](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shrine_of_Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h "Shrine of Bahá'u'lláh") in [Acre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acre,_Israel "Acre, Israel").<sup id="cite_ref-640"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-640">[631]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-641"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-641">[632]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-642"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-642">[633]</a></sup> The [Mahmood Mosque](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmood_Mosque,_Haifa "Mahmood Mosque, Haifa") is affiliated with the reformist [Ahmadiyya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmadiyya_in_Israel "Ahmadiyya in Israel") movement. [Kababir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kababir "Kababir"), Haifa's mixed neighbourhood of Jews and Ahmadi Arabs, is one of a few of its kind in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-643"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-643">[634]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-644"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-644">[635]</a></sup>

### Education

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Brain_research_labs-Bar_Ilan_university.jpg)

[Multidisciplinary Brain Research Center](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bar-Ilan_University#Leslie_and_Susan_Gonda_Multidisciplinary_Brain_Research_Center "Bar-Ilan University") at [Bar-Ilan University](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bar-Ilan_University "Bar-Ilan University")

Education is highly valued in the Israeli culture and was viewed as a [fundamental block of ancient Israelites](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_education_in_ancient_Israel_and_Judah "History of education in ancient Israel and Judah").<sup id="cite_ref-645"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-645">[636]</a></sup> In 2015, the country [ranked](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_tertiary_education_attainment "List of countries by tertiary education attainment") third among [OECD](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/OECD "OECD") members for the percentage of 25–64 year-olds that have attained [tertiary education](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tertiary_education "Tertiary education") with 49% compared with the OECD average of 35%.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_education_646-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-OECD_education-646">[637]</a></sup> In 2012, the country ranked third in the number of academic degrees per capita (20 percent of the population).<sup id="cite_ref-647"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-647">[638]</a></sup>

Israel has a [school life expectancy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/School_life_expectancy "School life expectancy") of 16 years and a [literacy rate](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_literacy_rate "List of countries by literacy rate") of 97.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_314-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-cia-314">[305]</a></sup> The State Education Law (1953) established five types of schools: state secular, state religious, ultra orthodox, communal settlement schools, and Arab schools. The public secular is the largest school group, and is attended by the majority of Jewish and non-Arab pupils. Most Arabs send their children to schools where Arabic is the language of instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-648"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-648">[639]</a></sup> Education is compulsory for children between the ages of three and eighteen.<sup id="cite_ref-649"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-649">[640]</a></sup> Schooling is divided into three tiers – primary school (grades 1–6), [middle school](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_school "Middle school") (grades 7–9), and high school (grades 10–12) – culminating with _[Bagrut](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bagrut "Bagrut")_ matriculation exams. Proficiency in core subjects such as mathematics, the [Hebrew language](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language "Hebrew language"), Hebrew and general literature, the [English language](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_language "English language"), history, Biblical scripture and civics is necessary to receive a Bagrut certificate.<sup id="cite_ref-moia_650-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-moia-650">[641]</a></sup>

Israel's Jewish population maintains a relatively high level of educational attainment where just under half of all Israeli Jews (46%) hold post-secondary degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-651"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-651">[642]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-652"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-652">[643]</a></sup> Israeli Jews (among those ages 25 and older) have average of 11.6 years of schooling making them one of the most highly educated of all major religious groups in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-653"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-653">[644]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-654"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-654">[645]</a></sup> In Arab, Christian and [Druze](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druze "Druze") schools, the exam on Biblical studies is replaced by an exam on Muslim, Christian or Druze heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-655"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-655">[646]</a></sup> In 2020, 68.7% of all Israeli twelfth graders earned a matriculation certificate.<sup id="cite_ref-656"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-656">[647]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MountScopusDec032022_03.jpg)

[Mount Scopus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Scopus "Mount Scopus") Campus of the [Hebrew University of Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem "Hebrew University of Jerusalem")

Israel has a tradition of higher education where its quality university education has been largely responsible in spurring the nation's modern economic development.<sup id="cite_ref-657"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-657">[648]</a></sup> Israel has [nine public universities subsidized by the state and 49 private colleges](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Israeli_universities_and_colleges "List of Israeli universities and colleges").<sup id="cite_ref-moia_650-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-moia-650">[641]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-658"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-658">[649]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-659"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-659">[650]</a></sup> The [Hebrew University of Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem "Hebrew University of Jerusalem") houses the [National Library of Israel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Library_of_Israel "National Library of Israel"), the world's largest repository of Judaica and Hebraica.<sup id="cite_ref-660"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-660">[651]</a></sup> The [Technion](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technion "Technion") and the Hebrew University consistently ranked among world's 100 top universities by [ARWU](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities "Academic Ranking of World Universities") ranking.<sup id="cite_ref-ARWU_540-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-ARWU-540">[531]</a></sup> Other major universities include the [Weizmann Institute of Science](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weizmann_Institute_of_Science "Weizmann Institute of Science"), [Tel Aviv University](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Aviv_University "Tel Aviv University"), [Ben-Gurion University of the Negev](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ben-Gurion_University_of_the_Negev "Ben-Gurion University of the Negev"), [Bar-Ilan University](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bar-Ilan_University "Bar-Ilan University"), the [University of Haifa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Haifa "University of Haifa") and the [Open University of Israel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_University_of_Israel "Open University of Israel").

## Culture

Israel's diverse culture stems from the diversity of its population: Jews from diaspora communities around the world brought their cultural and religious traditions back with them.<sup id="cite_ref-661"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-661">[652]</a></sup> Arab influences are present in many cultural spheres,<sup id="cite_ref-MendelRanta2016p137_662-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-MendelRanta2016p137-662">[653]</a></sup> such as [architecture](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Architecture_of_Israel "Architecture of Israel"),<sup id="cite_ref-663"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-663">[654]</a></sup> [music](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music_of_Israel "Music of Israel"),<sup id="cite_ref-664"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-664">[655]</a></sup> and [cuisine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_cuisine "Israeli cuisine").<sup id="cite_ref-665"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-665">[656]</a></sup> Israel is the only country where life revolves around the [Hebrew calendar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_calendar "Hebrew calendar"). [Holidays](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Israel "Public holidays in Israel") are determined by the [Jewish holidays](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_holiday "Jewish holiday"). The official day of rest is Saturday, the [Jewish Sabbath](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shabbat "Shabbat").<sup id="cite_ref-666"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-666">[657]</a></sup>

### Literature

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Agnon.jpg)

[Shmuel Yosef Agnon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shmuel_Yosef_Agnon "Shmuel Yosef Agnon"), laureate of the [Nobel Prize in Literature](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature "Nobel Prize in Literature")

[Israeli literature](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_literature "Israeli literature") is primarily [poetry](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_Hebrew_poetry "Modern Hebrew poetry") and prose written in [Hebrew](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language "Hebrew language"), as part of the [renaissance](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language "Revival of the Hebrew language") of Hebrew as a spoken language since the mid-19th century, although a small body of literature is published in other languages. By law, two copies of all printed matter published in Israel must be deposited in the [National Library of Israel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Library_of_Israel "National Library of Israel"). In 2001, the law was amended to include audio and video recordings, and other non-print media.<sup id="cite_ref-667"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-667">[658]</a></sup> In 2016, 89 percent of the 7,300 books transferred to the library were in Hebrew.<sup id="cite_ref-668"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-668">[659]</a></sup>

In 1966, [Shmuel Yosef Agnon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shmuel_Yosef_Agnon "Shmuel Yosef Agnon") shared the [Nobel Prize in Literature](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature "Nobel Prize in Literature") with German Jewish author [Nelly Sachs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nelly_Sachs "Nelly Sachs").<sup id="cite_ref-669"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-669">[660]</a></sup> Leading Israeli poets include [Yehuda Amichai](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yehuda_Amichai "Yehuda Amichai"), [Nathan Alterman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nathan_Alterman "Nathan Alterman"), [Leah Goldberg](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leah_Goldberg "Leah Goldberg"), and [Rachel Bluwstein](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rachel_Bluwstein "Rachel Bluwstein").<sup id="cite_ref-670"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-670">[661]</a></sup> Internationally famous contemporary Israeli novelists include [Amos Oz](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amos_Oz "Amos Oz"), [Etgar Keret](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Etgar_Keret "Etgar Keret") and [David Grossman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Grossman "David Grossman").<sup id="cite_ref-671"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-671">[662]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-672"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-672">[663]</a></sup>

### Music and dance

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Israel_Philharmonic_Orchestra.jpg)

[Israel Philharmonic Orchestra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_Philharmonic_Orchestra "Israel Philharmonic Orchestra") conducted by [Zubin Mehta](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zubin_Mehta "Zubin Mehta")

[Israeli music](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music_of_Israel "Music of Israel") includes [Mizrahi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mizrahi_music "Mizrahi music") and [Sephardic music](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sephardic_music "Sephardic music"), [Hasidic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism "Hasidic Judaism") melodies, [Greek music](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_music_in_Israel "Greek music in Israel"), [jazz](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jazz "Jazz"), and [pop rock](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pop_rock "Pop rock").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroughtonEllinghamTrillo1999365–369_673-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroughtonEllinghamTrillo1999365%E2%80%93369-673">[664]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-674"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-674">[665]</a></sup> The [Israel Philharmonic Orchestra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_Philharmonic_Orchestra "Israel Philharmonic Orchestra")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBen-Sasson19851095_675-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBen-Sasson19851095-675">[666]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-676"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-676">[667]</a></sup> has been in operation for over seventy years and performs more than two hundred concerts each year.<sup id="cite_ref-677"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-677">[668]</a></sup> [Itzhak Perlman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Itzhak_Perlman "Itzhak Perlman"), [Pinchas Zukerman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pinchas_Zukerman "Pinchas Zukerman") and [Ofra Haza](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ofra_Haza "Ofra Haza") are among the internationally acclaimed musicians born in Israel. [Israel has participated](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_in_the_Eurovision_Song_Contest "Israel in the Eurovision Song Contest") in the [Eurovision Song Contest](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest "Eurovision Song Contest") nearly every year since 1973, winning the competition four times and hosting it twice.<sup id="cite_ref-678"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-678">[669]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-679"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-679">[670]</a></sup> [Eilat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eilat "Eilat") has hosted its own international music festival, the [Red Sea Jazz Festival](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Sea_Jazz_Festival "Red Sea Jazz Festival"), every summer since 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-680"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-680">[671]</a></sup> The nation's canonical [folk songs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folk_music "Folk music") are known as "Songs of the Land of Israel".<sup id="cite_ref-681"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-681">[672]</a></sup>

### Cinema and theatre

Ten Israeli films [have been final nominees](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Israeli_submissions_for_the_Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film "List of Israeli submissions for the Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film") for [Best Foreign Language Film](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film "Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film") at the [Academy Awards](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academy_Awards "Academy Awards"). Palestinian Israeli filmmakers have made films dealing with the Arab-Israeli conflict and status of Palestinians within Israel, such as [Mohammed Bakri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_Bakri "Mohammed Bakri")'s 2002 film _[Jenin, Jenin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jenin,_Jenin "Jenin, Jenin")_ and _[The Syrian Bride](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Syrian_Bride "The Syrian Bride")_.

Continuing the strong theatrical traditions of the [Yiddish theatre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yiddish_theatre "Yiddish theatre") in Eastern Europe, Israel maintains a vibrant theatre scene. Founded in 1918, [Habima Theatre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habima_Theatre "Habima Theatre") in Tel Aviv is Israel's oldest [repertory theater](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repertory_theater "Repertory theater") company and national theater.<sup id="cite_ref-682"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-682">[673]</a></sup> Other theatres include [Ohel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ohel_Theater "Ohel Theater"), [the Cameri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cameri_Theatre "Cameri Theatre") and [Gesher](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gesher_Theater "Gesher Theater").<sup id="cite_ref-683"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-683">[674]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-684"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-684">[675]</a></sup>

### Arts

Israeli Jewish art has been particularly influenced by the [Kabbalah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kabbalah "Kabbalah"), the [Talmud](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talmud "Talmud") and the [Zohar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zohar "Zohar"). Another art movement that held a prominent role in the 20th century was the [School of Paris](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/School_of_Paris "School of Paris"). In the late 19th and early 20th century, the [Yishuv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yishuv "Yishuv")'s art was dominated by art trends emanating [Bezalel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bezalel_Academy_of_Arts_and_Design "Bezalel Academy of Arts and Design"). Beginning in the 1920s, the local art scene was heavily influenced by modern French art, first introduced by [Isaac Frenkel Frenel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yitzhak_Frenkel "Yitzhak Frenkel").<sup id="cite_ref-685"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-685">[676]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-686"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-686">[677]</a></sup> Jewish masters of the [school of Paris](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/School_of_Paris "School of Paris"), such as [Soutine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cha%C3%AFm_Soutine "Chaïm Soutine"), [Kikoine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michel_Kikoine "Michel Kikoine"), [Frenkel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yitzhak_Frenkel "Yitzhak Frenkel"), [Chagall](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marc_Chagall "Marc Chagall") heavily influenced the subsequent development of Israeli art.<sup id="cite_ref-687"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-687">[678]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:43_688-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-:43-688">[679]</a></sup> Israeli sculpture took inspiration from modern [European sculpture](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art_of_Europe "Art of Europe") as well [Mesopotamian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art_of_Mesopotamia "Art of Mesopotamia"), [Assyrian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assyrian_sculpture "Assyrian sculpture") and local art.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_689-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-:6-689">[680]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-690"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-690">[681]</a></sup> [Avraham Melnikov](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avraham_Melnikov "Avraham Melnikov")'s roaring lion, David Polus' Alexander Zaid and [Ze'ev Ben Zvi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zeev_Ben-Zvi "Zeev Ben-Zvi")'s cubist sculpture exemplify some of the different streams in Israeli sculpture.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_689-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-:6-689">[680]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-691"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-691">[682]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-692"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-692">[683]</a></sup>

Common themes in Israeli art are the mystical cities of [Safed](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safed "Safed") and [Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem "Jerusalem"), the bohemian café culture of [Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Aviv "Tel Aviv"), agricultural landscapes, biblical stories and war. Today Israeli art has delved into [Optical art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_art "Optical art"), [AI art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_art "Artificial intelligence art"), [digital art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_art "Digital art") and the use of salt in sculpture.<sup id="cite_ref-:43_688-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-:43-688">[679]</a></sup>

### Architecture

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:%D7%9E%D7%95%D7%96%D7%99%D7%90%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%94%D7%91%D7%90%D7%95%D7%94%D7%90%D7%95%D7%A11.jpg)

[Bauhaus Museum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bauhaus_Foundation_Tel_Aviv "Bauhaus Foundation Tel Aviv"), [Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Aviv "Tel Aviv")

Due to the immigration of Jewish architects, architecture in Israel has come to reflect different styles. In the early 20th century Jewish architects sought to combine Occidental and Oriental architecture producing buildings that showcase a myriad of infused styles.<sup id="cite_ref-693"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-693">[684]</a></sup> The [eclectic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eclecticism_in_architecture "Eclecticism in architecture") style gave way to the modernist [Bauhaus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bauhaus "Bauhaus") style with the influx of German Jewish architects (among them [Erich Mendelsohn](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erich_Mendelsohn "Erich Mendelsohn")) fleeing [Nazi persecution](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_persecution_of_Jews "Nazi persecution of Jews").<sup id="cite_ref-694"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-694">[685]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-695"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-695">[686]</a></sup> The [White City of Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_City_(Tel_Aviv) "White City (Tel Aviv)") is a [UNESCO heritage site](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNESCO_Heritage_site "UNESCO Heritage site").<sup id="cite_ref-696"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-696">[687]</a></sup> Following independence, multiple government projects were commissioned, a grand part built in a brutalist style with heavy emphasis on the use of concrete and acclimatization to the Israel's desert climate.<sup id="cite_ref-697"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-697">[688]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-698"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-698">[689]</a></sup>

Several novel ideas such as the [Garden City](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Garden_city_movement "Garden city movement") were implemented Israeli cities; the [Geddes plan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geddes_Plan_for_Tel_Aviv "Geddes Plan for Tel Aviv") of Tel Aviv became renowned internationally for its revolutionary design and adaptation to the local climate.<sup id="cite_ref-699"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-699">[690]</a></sup> The design of kibbutzim also came to reflect ideology, such as the planning of the circular kibbutz [Nahalal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nahalal "Nahalal") by [Richard Kauffmann](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Kauffmann "Richard Kauffmann").<sup id="cite_ref-700"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-700">[691]</a></sup>

### Media

The 2017 _[Freedom of the Press](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_of_the_Press_(report) "Freedom of the Press (report)")_ annual report by [Freedom House](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_House "Freedom House") ranked Israel as the [Middle East and North Africa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/MENA "MENA")'s most free country, and 64th globally.<sup id="cite_ref-701"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-701">[692]</a></sup> In the 2017 [Press Freedom Index](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index "Press Freedom Index") by [Reporters Without Borders](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders "Reporters Without Borders"), Israel (including "Israel extraterritorial" since 2013 ranking)<sup id="cite_ref-702"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-702">[693]</a></sup> was placed 91st of 180 countries, first in the Middle East and North Africa region.<sup id="cite_ref-703"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-703">[694]</a></sup> Reporters Without Borders noted that "Palestinian journalists are systematically subjected to violence as a result of their coverage of events in the West Bank".<sup id="cite_ref-704"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-704">[695]</a></sup> More than fifty Palestinian journalists have been killed by Israel since 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-Guyer_2022_705-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-Guyer_2022-705">[696]</a></sup>

### Museums

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Billy_Rose_Art_Garden_(14755133799).jpg)

[Shrine of the Book](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shrine_of_the_Book "Shrine of the Book"), repository of the [Dead Sea Scrolls](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls "Dead Sea Scrolls") in Jerusalem

The [Israel Museum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_Museum "Israel Museum") in Jerusalem is one of Israel's most important cultural institutions<sup id="cite_ref-imj_706-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-imj-706">[697]</a></sup> and houses the [Dead Sea Scrolls](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls "Dead Sea Scrolls"),<sup id="cite_ref-707"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-707">[698]</a></sup> along with an extensive collection of [Judaica](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaica "Judaica") and [European art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_art "European art").<sup id="cite_ref-imj_706-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-imj-706">[697]</a></sup> Israel's national [Holocaust](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Holocaust "The Holocaust") museum, [Yad Vashem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yad_Vashem "Yad Vashem"), is the world central archive of Holocaust-related information.<sup id="cite_ref-708"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-708">[699]</a></sup> [ANU - Museum of the Jewish People](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ANU_-_Museum_of_the_Jewish_People "ANU - Museum of the Jewish People") on the campus of [Tel Aviv University](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Aviv_University "Tel Aviv University"), is an interactive museum devoted to the history of Jewish communities around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-709"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-709">[700]</a></sup>

Israel has the highest number of museums per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-710"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-710">[701]</a></sup> Several Israeli museums are devoted to Islamic culture, including the [Rockefeller Museum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rockefeller_Museum "Rockefeller Museum") and the [L. A. Mayer Institute for Islamic Art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/L._A._Mayer_Institute_for_Islamic_Art "L. A. Mayer Institute for Islamic Art"), both in Jerusalem. The Rockefeller specializes in archaeological remains from Middle East history. It is also the home of the first [hominid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominid "Hominid") fossil skull found in Western Asia, called [Galilee Man](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galilee_Man "Galilee Man").<sup id="cite_ref-711"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-711">[702]</a></sup>

### Cuisine

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Food_in_Israel.jpg)

A meal including [falafel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falafel "Falafel"), [hummus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hummus "Hummus"), [French fries](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_fries "French fries") and [Israeli salad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_salad "Israeli salad")

[Israeli cuisine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_cuisine "Israeli cuisine") includes local dishes as well as [Jewish cuisine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_cuisine "Jewish cuisine") brought to the country by immigrants. Particularly since the late 1970s, an Israeli [fusion cuisine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fusion_cuisine "Fusion cuisine") has developed.<sup id="cite_ref-raviv_712-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-raviv-712">[703]</a></sup> Israeli cuisine has adopted, and continues to adapt, elements of the [Mizrahi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuisine_of_the_Mizrahi_Jews "Cuisine of the Mizrahi Jews"), [Sephardi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuisine_of_the_Sephardic_Jews "Cuisine of the Sephardic Jews"), and [Ashkenazi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashkenazi_cuisine "Ashkenazi cuisine") styles of cooking. It incorporates many foods traditionally eaten in the [Levantine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levantine_cuisine "Levantine cuisine"), [Arab](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_cuisine "Arab cuisine"), [Middle Eastern](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Eastern_cuisine "Middle Eastern cuisine") and [Mediterranean](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean_cuisine "Mediterranean cuisine") cuisines, such as [falafel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falafel "Falafel"), [hummus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hummus "Hummus"), [shakshouka](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shakshouka "Shakshouka"), [couscous](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Couscous "Couscous"), and [za'atar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Za%27atar "Za'atar"). [Schnitzel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schnitzel "Schnitzel"), [pizza](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pizza "Pizza"), [hamburgers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamburger "Hamburger"), [French fries](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_fries "French fries"), [rice](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice "Rice") and [salad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salad "Salad") are common in Israel.

Roughly half of the Israeli-Jewish population attests to keeping [kosher](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosher "Kosher") at home.<sup id="cite_ref-713"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-713">[704]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bernstein_227,_233–234_714-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-bernstein_227,_233%E2%80%93234-714">[705]</a></sup> [Kosher restaurants](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosher_restaurant "Kosher restaurant") make up around a quarter of the total as of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-raviv_712-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-raviv-712">[703]</a></sup> Together with non-kosher fish, rabbits and ostriches, [pork](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pork "Pork")—often called "white meat" in Israel<sup id="cite_ref-bernstein-231_715-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-bernstein-231-715">[706]</a></sup>—is produced and consumed, though [it is forbidden](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religious_restrictions_on_the_consumption_of_pork "Religious restrictions on the consumption of pork") by both Judaism and Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-716"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-716">[707]</a></sup>

### Sports

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:%D7%99%D7%A8%D7%95%D7%A7_%D7%A2%D7%95%D7%9C%D7%94.jpg)

[Maccabi Haifa F.C.](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maccabi_Haifa_F.C. "Maccabi Haifa F.C.") fans at [Sammy Ofer Stadium](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sammy_Ofer_Stadium "Sammy Ofer Stadium") in the city of Haifa

The most popular spectator sports in Israel are [association football](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Association_football "Association football") and [basketball](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basketball "Basketball").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorstrick2004141_717-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorstrick2004141-717">[708]</a></sup> The [Israeli Premier League](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_Premier_League "Israeli Premier League") is the country's premier football league, and the [Israeli Basketball Premier League](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_Basketball_Premier_League "Israeli Basketball Premier League") is the premier basketball league.<sup id="cite_ref-718"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-718">[709]</a></sup> [Maccabi Haifa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maccabi_Haifa_F.C. "Maccabi Haifa F.C."), [Maccabi Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maccabi_Tel_Aviv_F.C. "Maccabi Tel Aviv F.C."), [Hapoel Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hapoel_Tel_Aviv_F.C. "Hapoel Tel Aviv F.C.") and [Beitar Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beitar_Jerusalem_F.C. "Beitar Jerusalem F.C.") are the largest [football clubs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_football_clubs_in_Israel "List of football clubs in Israel"). Maccabi Tel Aviv, Maccabi Haifa and Hapoel Tel Aviv have competed in the [UEFA Champions League](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League "UEFA Champions League") and Hapoel Tel Aviv reached the [UEFA Cup](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UEFA_Cup "UEFA Cup") quarter-finals. Israel hosted and won the [1964 AFC Asian Cup](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1964_AFC_Asian_Cup "1964 AFC Asian Cup"); in 1970 the [Israel national football team](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_national_football_team "Israel national football team") qualified for the [FIFA World Cup](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1970_FIFA_World_Cup "1970 FIFA World Cup"), the only time it participated in the World Cup. The [1974 Asian Games](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1974_Asian_Games "1974 Asian Games"), held in Tehran, were the last Asian Games in which Israel [participated](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_at_the_Asian_Games "Israel at the Asian Games"), plagued by Arab countries that [refused](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boycotts_of_Israel_in_sports "Boycotts of Israel in sports") to compete with Israel. Israel was excluded from the [1978 Asian Games](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1978_Asian_Games "1978 Asian Games") and since then has not competed in Asian sport events.<sup id="cite_ref-719"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-719">[710]</a></sup> In 1994, [UEFA](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UEFA "UEFA") agreed to admit Israel, and its football teams now compete in Europe. [Maccabi Tel Aviv B.C.](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maccabi_Tel_Aviv_B.C. "Maccabi Tel Aviv B.C.") has won the [European championship](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/FIBA_European_Champions_Cup_and_EuroLeague_records_and_statistics "FIBA European Champions Cup and EuroLeague records and statistics") in basketball six times.<sup id="cite_ref-720"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-720">[711]</a></sup>

Israel has won [nine Olympic medals](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_at_the_Olympics "Israel at the Olympics") since its first win [in 1992](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1992_Summer_Olympics "1992 Summer Olympics"), including a gold medal in [windsurfing](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sailing_at_the_2004_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_Mistral_One_Design "Sailing at the 2004 Summer Olympics – Men's Mistral One Design") at the [2004 Summer Olympics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2004_Summer_Olympics "2004 Summer Olympics").<sup id="cite_ref-721"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-721">[712]</a></sup> Israel has won [over 100](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_at_the_Paralympics "Israel at the Paralympics") gold medals in the [Paralympic Games](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paralympic_Games "Paralympic Games") and is ranked 20th in the [all-time medal count](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/All-time_Paralympic_Games_medal_table "All-time Paralympic Games medal table"). The [1968 Summer Paralympics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1968_Summer_Paralympics "1968 Summer Paralympics") were hosted by Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-722"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-722">[713]</a></sup> The [Maccabiah Games](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maccabiah_Games "Maccabiah Games"), an Olympic-style event for [Jewish](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Jews_in_sports "List of Jews in sports") and Israeli athletes, was inaugurated in the 1930s, and has been held every four years since then. [Krav Maga](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Krav_Maga "Krav Maga"), a martial art developed by Jewish ghetto defenders during the struggle against [fascism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fascism "Fascism") in Europe, is used by the Israeli security forces and police. Its effectiveness and practical approach to self-defense, have won it widespread admiration and adherence around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-723"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-723">[714]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:BorisGelfandSicilianDefence.jpg)

[Boris Gelfand](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Gelfand "Boris Gelfand"), chess [Grandmaster](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grandmaster_(chess) "Grandmaster (chess)")

[Chess](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess "Chess") is a leading sport in Israel. There are many Israeli grandmasters and [Israeli chess players](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Israeli_chess_players "List of Israeli chess players") have won a number of youth world championships.<sup id="cite_ref-724"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-724">[715]</a></sup> Israel stages an annual international [championship](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_Chess_Championship "Israeli Chess Championship") and hosted the [World Team Chess Championship](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Team_Chess_Championship "World Team Chess Championship") in 2005. The Ministry of Education and the [World Chess Federation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/FIDE "FIDE") agreed upon a project of teaching chess within Israeli schools, and it has been introduced into the curriculum of some schools.<sup id="cite_ref-725"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-725">[716]</a></sup> The city of [Beersheba](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beersheba "Beersheba") has become a national chess center, with the game being taught in its kindergartens. Owing partly to Soviet immigration, it is home to the largest number of [chess grandmasters](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grandmaster_(chess) "Grandmaster (chess)") of any city in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-726"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-726">[717]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-727"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-727">[718]</a></sup> The Israeli chess team won the silver medal at the [2008 Chess Olympiad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/38th_Chess_Olympiad "38th Chess Olympiad")<sup id="cite_ref-728"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-728">[719]</a></sup> and the bronze, coming in third among 148 teams, at the [2010 Olympiad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/39th_Chess_Olympiad "39th Chess Olympiad"). Israeli grandmaster [Boris Gelfand](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Gelfand "Boris Gelfand") won the [Chess World Cup 2009](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess_World_Cup_2009 "Chess World Cup 2009").<sup id="cite_ref-729"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-729">[720]</a></sup>

### Notes

1.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-7 "Jump up")** Recognition by other UN member states: Russia ([West Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Jerusalem "West Jerusalem")),<sup id="cite_ref-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> the [Czech Republic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czech_Republic "Czech Republic") ([West Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Jerusalem "West Jerusalem")),<sup id="cite_ref-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> [Honduras](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honduras "Honduras"),<sup id="cite_ref-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> [Guatemala](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guatemala "Guatemala"),<sup id="cite_ref-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> [Nauru](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nauru "Nauru"),<sup id="cite_ref-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
2.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-9 "Jump up")** Jerusalem is Israel's largest city if including [East Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Jerusalem "East Jerusalem"), which is widely recognized as occupied territory.<sup id="cite_ref-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> If East Jeruslem is not counted, the largest city would be [Tel Aviv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Aviv "Tel Aviv").
3.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-14 "Jump up")** Arabic has a "special status" as set by the [Basic Law of 2018](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_Law:_Israel_as_the_Nation-State_of_the_Jewish_People "Basic Law: Israel as the Nation-State of the Jewish People"), which allows it to be used by official institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-lang2_11-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-lang2-11">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lang3_12-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-lang3-12">[10]</a></sup> Prior to that law's passage, Arabic had been an official language alongside Hebrew.<sup id="cite_ref-lang1_13-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-lang1-13">[11]</a></sup>
4.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-oecd_22-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-oecd_22-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-oecd_22-2) Israeli population and economic data covers the economic territory of Israel, including the Golan Heights, East Jerusalem and Israeli settlements in the West Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOECD2011_368-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOECD2011-368">[359]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-369">[360]</a></sup>
5.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-71 "Jump up")** The [personal name "Israel"](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel_(name) "Israel (name)") appears much earlier, in material from [Ebla](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ebla "Ebla").<sup id="cite_ref-70"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup>

1.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-26 "Jump up")** ; [Hebrew](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language "Hebrew language"): יִשְׂרָאֵל _Yīsrāʾēl_ [\[jisʁaˈʔel\]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew "Help:IPA/Hebrew"); [Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language"): إِسْرَائِيل _ʾIsrāʾīl_
2.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-27 "Jump up")** [Hebrew](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language "Hebrew language"): מְדִינַת יִשְׂרָאֵל _Medīnat Yīsrāʾēl_ [\[mediˈnat jisʁaˈʔel\]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew "Help:IPA/Hebrew"); [Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language"): دَوْلَة إِسْرَائِيل _Dawlat Isrāʾīl_
3.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-30 "Jump up")** The [Jerusalem Law](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem_Law "Jerusalem Law") states that "Jerusalem, complete and united, is the capital of Israel" and the city serves as the seat of the government, home to the President's residence, government offices, supreme court, and [parliament](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knesset "Knesset"). [United Nations Security Council Resolution 478](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_478 "United Nations Security Council Resolution 478") (20 August 1980; 14–0, U.S. abstaining) declared the Jerusalem Law "null and void" and called on member states to withdraw their diplomatic missions from Jerusalem.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKellerman1993140_29-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKellerman1993140-29">[22]</a></sup> See [Status of Jerusalem](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Status_of_Jerusalem "Status of Jerusalem") for more information.

23.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-31 "Jump up")** Akram, Susan M., Michael Dumper, Michael Lynk, and Iain Scobbie, eds. 2010. _International Law and the Israeli-Palestinian Conflict: A Rights-Based Approach to Middle East Peace_. Routledge. p. 119: "UN General Assembly Resolution 181 recommended the creation of an international zone, or corpus separatum, in Jerusalem to be administered by the UN for a 10-year period, after which there would be a referendum to determine its future. This approach applies equally to West and East Jerusalem and is not affected by the occupation of East Jerusalem in 1967. To a large extent it is this approach that still guides the diplomatic behaviour of states and thus has greater force in international law."

32.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-40 "Jump up")** Meir-Glitzenstein, Esther (Fall 2018). "Turning Points in the Historiography of Jewish Immigration from Arab Countries to Israel". _Israel Studies_. **23** (3). Indiana University Press: 114–122. [doi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.2979/israelstudies.23.3.15](https://doi.org/10.2979%2Fisraelstudies.23.3.15). [JSTOR](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier) "JSTOR (identifier)") [10.2979/israelstudies.23.3.15](https://www.jstor.org/stable/10.2979/israelstudies.23.3.15). [S2CID](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/S2CID_(identifier) "S2CID (identifier)") [150208821](https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:150208821). The mass immigration from Arab countries began in mid-1949 and included three communities that relocated to Israel almost in their entirety: 31,000 Jews from Libya, 50,000 from Yemen, and 125,000 from Iraq. Additional immigrants arrived from Egypt, Morocco, Tunisia, Turkey, Iran, India, and elsewhere. Within three years, the Jewish population of Israel doubled. The ethnic composition of the population shifted as well, as immigrants from Muslim counties and their offspring now comprised one third of the Jewish population—an unprecedented phenomenon in global immigration history. From 1952–60, Israel regulated and restricted immigration from Muslim countries with a selective immigration policy based on economic criteria, and sent these immigrants, most of whom were North African, to peripheral Israeli settlements. The selective immigration policy ended in 1961 when, following an agreement between Israel and Morocco, about 100,000 Jews immigrated to the State. From 1952–68 about 600,000 Jews arrived in Israel, three quarters of whom were from Arab countries and the remaining immigrants were largely from Eastern Europe. Today fewer than 30,000 remain in Muslim countries, mostly concentrated in Iran and Turkey.

62.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-70 "Jump up")** Hasel, Michael G. (1 January 1994). "Israel in the Merneptah Stela". _Bulletin of the American Schools of Oriental Research_. **296** (296): 45–61. [doi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.2307/1357179](https://doi.org/10.2307%2F1357179). [JSTOR](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier) "JSTOR (identifier)") [1357179](https://www.jstor.org/stable/1357179). [S2CID](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/S2CID_(identifier) "S2CID (identifier)") [164052192](https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:164052192).  
    \* Bertman, Stephen (14 July 2005). [_Handbook to Life in Ancient Mesopotamia_](https://books.google.com/books?id=1C4NKp4zgIQC&q=ebla%20israel%20ishmael%20abraham&pg=PA317). Oxford University Press. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-19-518364-1](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-518364-1 "Special:BookSources/978-0-19-518364-1").  
    \* Meindert Dijkstra (2010). "Origins of Israel between history and ideology". In [Becking, Bob](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lester_L._Grabbe "Lester L. Grabbe"); Grabbe, Lester (eds.). _Between Evidence and Ideology Essays on the History of Ancient Israel read at the Joint Meeting of the Society for Old Testament Study and the Oud Testamentisch Werkgezelschap Lincoln, July 2009_. Brill. p. 47. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-90-04-18737-5](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-90-04-18737-5 "Special:BookSources/978-90-04-18737-5"). As a West Semitic personal name it existed long before it became a tribal or a geographical name. This is not without significance, though is it rarely mentioned. We learn of a maryanu named ysr"il (\*Yi¡sr—a"ilu) from Ugarit living in the same period, but the name was already used a thousand years before in Ebla. The word Israel originated as a West Semitic personal name. One of the many names that developed into the name of the ancestor of a clan, of a tribe and finally of a people and a nation.

65.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-74 "Jump up")** Mark Smith in "The Early History of God: Yahweh and Other Deities of Ancient Israel" states "Despite the long regnant model that the Canaanites and Israelites were people of fundamentally different culture, archaeological data now casts doubt on this view. The material culture of the region exhibits numerous common points between Israelites and Canaanites in the Iron I period (c. 1200–1000 BCE). The record would suggest that the Israelite culture largely overlapped with and derived from Canaanite culture... In short, Israelite culture was largely Canaanite in nature. Given the information available, one cannot maintain a radical cultural separation between Canaanites and Israelites for the Iron I period." (pp. 6–7). Smith, Mark (2002) "The Early History of God: Yahweh and Other Deities of Ancient Israel" (Eerdman's)

95.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-104 "Jump up")** Helyer, Larry R.; McDonald, Lee Martin (2013). "The Hasmoneans and the Hasmonean Era". In Green, Joel B.; McDonald, Lee Martin (eds.). _The World of the New Testament: Cultural, Social, and Historical Contexts_. Baker Academic. pp. 45–47. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-8010-9861-1](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8010-9861-1 "Special:BookSources/978-0-8010-9861-1"). [OCLC](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/OCLC_(identifier) "OCLC (identifier)") [961153992](https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/961153992). The ensuing power struggle left Hyrcanus with a free hand in Judea, and he quickly reasserted Jewish sovereignty... Hyrcanus then engaged in a series of military campaigns aimed at territorial expansion. He first conquered areas in the Transjordan. He then turned his attention to Samaria, which had long separated Judea from the northern Jewish settlements in Lower Galilee. In the south, Adora and Marisa were conquered; (Aristobulus') primary accomplishment was annexing and Judaizing the region of Iturea, located between the Lebanon and Anti-Lebanon mountains
96.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-105 "Jump up")** Ben-Sasson, H.H. (1976). [_A History of the Jewish People_](https://books.google.com/books?id=2kSovzudhFUC&pg=PA226). Harvard University Press. p. 226. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-674-39731-6](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-674-39731-6 "Special:BookSources/978-0-674-39731-6"). The expansion of Hasmonean Judea took place gradually. Under Jonathan, Judea annexed southern Samaria and began to expand in the direction of the coast plain... The main ethnic changes were the work of John Hyrcanus... it was in his days and those of his son Aristobulus that the annexation of Idumea, Samaria and Galilee and the consolidation of Jewish settlement in Trans-Jordan was completed. Alexander Jannai, continuing the work of his predecessors, expanded Judean rule to the entire coastal plain, from the Carmel to the Egyptian border... and to additional areas in Trans-Jordan, including some of the Greek cities there.
97.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-106 "Jump up")** Ben-Eliyahu, Eyal (30 April 2019). [_Identity and Territory: Jewish Perceptions of Space in Antiquity_](https://books.google.com/books?id=ZSyDDwAAQBAJ&pg=PA13). Univ of California Press. p. 13. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-520-29360-1](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-520-29360-1 "Special:BookSources/978-0-520-29360-1"). [OCLC](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/OCLC_(identifier) "OCLC (identifier)") [1103519319](https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/1103519319). From the beginning of the Second Temple period until the Muslim conquest—the land was part of imperial space. This was true from the early Persian period, as well as the time of Ptolemy and the Seleucids. The only exception was the Hasmonean Kingdom, with its sovereign Jewish rule—first over Judah and later, in Alexander Jannaeus's prime, extending to the coast, the north, and the eastern banks of the Jordan.
98.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-:8_107-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-:8_107-1) Schwartz, Seth (2014). [_The ancient Jews from Alexander to Muhammad_](https://books.google.com/books?id=Y6pkAwAAQBAJ&pg=PA85). Cambridge University Press. pp. 85–86. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-1-107-04127-1](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-04127-1 "Special:BookSources/978-1-107-04127-1"). [OCLC](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/OCLC_(identifier) "OCLC (identifier)") [863044259](https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/863044259). The year 70 ce marked transformations in demography, politics, Jewish civic status, Palestinian and more general Jewish economic and social structures, Jewish religious life beyond the sacrificial cult, and even Roman politics and the topography of the city of Rome itself. \[...\] The Revolt's failure had, to begin with, a demographic impact on the Jews of Palestine; many died in battle and as a result of siege conditions, not only in Jerusalem. \[...\] As indicated above, the figures for captives are conceivably more reliable. If 97,000 is roughly correct as a total for the war, it would mean that a huge percentage of the population was removed from the country, or at the very least displaced from their homes. Nevertheless, only sixty years later, there was a large enough population in the Judaean countryside to stage a massively disruptive second rebellion; this one appears to have ended, in 135, with devastation and depopulation of the district.
99.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-108 "Jump up")** Werner Eck, "Sklaven und Freigelassene von Römern in Iudaea und den angrenzenden Provinzen", Novum Testamentum 55 (2013): 1–21
100.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-109 "Jump up")** Raviv, Dvir; Ben David, Chaim (2021). ["Cassius Dio's figures for the demographic consequences of the Bar Kokhba War: Exaggeration or reliable account?"](https://doi.org/10.1017%2FS1047759421000271). _Journal of Roman Archaeology_. **34** (2): 585–607. [doi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1017/S1047759421000271](https://doi.org/10.1017%2FS1047759421000271). [ISSN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISSN_(identifier) "ISSN (identifier)") [1047-7594](https://www.worldcat.org/issn/1047-7594). [S2CID](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/S2CID_(identifier) "S2CID (identifier)") [245512193](https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:245512193). Scholars have long doubted the historical accuracy of Cassius Dio's account of the consequences of the Bar Kokhba War (Roman History 69.14). According to this text, considered the most reliable literary source for the Second Jewish Revolt, the war encompassed all of Judea: the Romans destroyed 985 villages and 50 fortresses, and killed 580,000 rebels. This article reassesses Cassius Dio's figures by drawing on new evidence from excavations and surveys in Judea, Transjordan, and the Galilee. Three research methods are combined: an ethno-archaeological comparison with the settlement picture in the Ottoman Period, comparison with similar settlement studies in the Galilee, and an evaluation of settled sites from the Middle Roman Period (70–136 CE). The study demonstrates the potential contribution of the archaeological record to this issue and supports the view of Cassius Dio's demographic data as a reliable account, which he based on contemporaneous documentation.
101.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-:02_110-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-:02_110-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel#cite_ref-:02_110-2) Mor, Menahem (18 April 2016). _The Second Jewish Revolt_. BRILL. pp. 483–484. [doi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1163/9789004314634](https://doi.org/10.1163%2F9789004314634). [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-90-04-31463-4](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-90-04-31463-4 "Special:BookSources/978-90-04-31463-4"). Land confiscation in Judaea was part of the suppression of the revolt policy of the Romans and punishment for the rebels. But the very claim that the sikarikon laws were annulled for settlement purposes seems to indicate that Jews continued to reside in Judaea even after the Second Revolt. There is no doubt that this area suffered the severest damage from the suppression of the revolt. Settlements in Judaea, such as Herodion and Bethar, had already been destroyed during the course of the revolt, and Jews were expelled from the districts of Gophna, Herodion, and Aqraba. However, it should not be claimed that the region of Judaea was completely destroyed. Jews continued to live in areas such as Lod (Lydda), south of the Hebron Mountain, and the coastal regions. In other areas of the Land of Israel that did not have any direct connection with the Second Revolt, no settlement changes can be identified as resulting from it.
